deprecate_posargs(deprecation_warning, remappable_names, /):\\n102:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n103:     Function/method decorator to deprecate some or all positional arguments.\\n104: \\n105:     The arguments: a RemovedInDjangoXXWarning warning\\n109:     category and a list of parameter names that have been changed from\\n110:     positional-or-keyword to keyword-only, in their original positional order.\\n111: \\n112:     Works on both functions and methods. To apply to a class constructor,\\n113:     decorate its __init__() method. To apply to a staticmethod or classmethod,\\n114:     use @deprecate_posargs after @staticmethod or @classmethod.\\n115: \\n116:     Example: to deprecate passing option1 or option2 as posargs, change::\\n117: \\n118:         def some_func(request, option1, option2=True):\\n119:             ...\\n120: \\n121:     to::\\n122: \\n123:         @deprecate_posargs(RemovedInDjangoXXWarning, [\\\"option1\\\", \\\"option2\\\"])\\n124:         def some_func(request, *, option1, option2=True):\\n125:             ...\\n126: \\n127:     After the deprecation period, remove the decorator (but keep the ``*``)::\\n128: \\n129:         def some_func(request, *, option1, option2=True):\\n130:             ...\\n131: \\n132:     Caution: during the deprecation period, do not add any new *positional*\\n133:     parameters or change the remaining ones. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01G5sXi3vVG6FdJaRfCxBxfN","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: ==========\\n2: Middleware\\n3: ==========\\n4: \\n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\\n6: It's a light, low-level \\\"plugin\\\" system for globally altering Django's input\\n7: or output.\\n8: \\n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\\n13: \\n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\\n18: \\n19: Writing your own middleware\\n20: ===========================\\n21: \\n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\\n25: \\n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\\n27: \\n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n30: \\n31:         def middleware(request):\\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n34: \\n35:             response = get_response(request)\\n36: \\n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n38:             # the view is called.\\n39: \\n40:             return response\\n41: \\n42:         return middleware\\n43: \\n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\\n45: \\n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n50: \\n51:         def __call__(self, request):\\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n54: \\n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\\n56: \\n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n58:             # the view is called.\\n59: \\n60:             return response\\n61: \\n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\\n66: \\n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\\n73: \\n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\\n77: \\n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\\n79: \\n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\\n81: --------------------------\\n82: \\n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\\n85: caveats:\\n86: \\n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\\n89: \\n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\\n92: \\n93: Marking middleware as unused\\n94: ----------------------------\\n95: \\n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\\n101: \\n102: Activating middleware\\n103: =====================\\n104: \\n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\\n106: your Django settings.\\n107: \\n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\\n112: \\n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\\n114:         \\\"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\\\",\\n115:         \\\"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\\\",\\n116:         \\\"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\\\",\\n117:         \\\"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\\\",\\n118:         \\\"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\\\",\\n119:         \\\"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\\\",\\n120:         \\\"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\\\",\\n121:     ]\\n122: \\n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\\n126: \\n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\\n128: other middleware. For instance,\\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\\n133: middleware classes.\\n134: \\n135: Middleware order and layering\\n136: =============================\\n137: \\n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\\n140: \\n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \\\"layer\\\" that\\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\\n146: out.\\n147: \\n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\\n152: \\n153: Other middleware hooks\\n154: ======================\\n155: \\n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\\n158: \\n159: .. _view-middleware:\\n160: \\n161: ``process_view()``\\n162: ------------------\\n163: \\n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\\n165: \\n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\\n172: (``request``).\\n173: \\n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\\n175: \\n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\\n182: \\n183: .. note::\\n184: \\n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\\n190: \\n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\\n196: \\n197: .. _exception-middleware:\\n198: \\n199: ``process_exception()``\\n200: -----------------------\\n201: \\n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\\n203: \\n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\\n206: \\n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\\n213: \\n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\\n218: \\n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\\n220: \\n221: ``process_template_response()``\\n222: -------------------------------\\n223: \\n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\\n225: \\n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\\n229: \\n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n233: \\n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n238: \\n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\\n241: called.\\n242: \\n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\\n245: \\n246: Dealing with streaming responses\\n247: ================================\\n248: \\n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\\n254: \\n255:     if response.streaming:\\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\\n257:     else:\\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\\n259: \\n260: .. note::\\n261: \\n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\\n265: \\n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\\n267:             for chunk in content:\\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\\n269: \\n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\\n274: support both types of iterator.\\n275: \\n276: Exception handling\\n277: ==================\\n278: \\n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\\n283: \\n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\\n294: \\n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\\n297: \\n298: .. _async-middleware:\\n299: \\n300: Asynchronous support\\n301: ====================\\n302: \\n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\\n306: \\n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\\n310: \\n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\\n313: \\n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\\n316: \\n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\\n322: \\n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\\n328: \\n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\\n332: \\n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\\n337: \\n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\\n339: \\n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\\n348: \\n349:             async def middleware(request):\\n350:                 # Do something here!\\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\\n352:                 return response\\n353: \\n354:         else:\\n355: \\n356:             def middleware(request):\\n357:                 # Do something here!\\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\\n359:                 return response\\n360: \\n361:         return middleware\\n362: \\n363: .. note::\\n364: \\n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\\n369: \\n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\\n372: \\n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\\n375: \\n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\\n377: \\n378: \\n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\\n380:         async_capable = True\\n381:         sync_capable = False\\n382: \\n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\\n387: \\n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\\n390:             # Some logic ...\\n391:             return response\\n392: \\n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\\n394: \\n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\\n396: ==========================================\\n397: \\n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\\n399:     :module:\\n400: \\n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\\n405: \\n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\\n408: \\n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\\n410: \\n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\\n413:    middleware and the view.\\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\\n415: #. Returns the response.\\n416: \\n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\\n419: directly.\\n420: \\n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\\n426: \\n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\\n429: \\n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\\n437:    response.\\n438: \\n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\\n445:    to the next middleware.\\n446: \\n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\\n455:    exception.\\n456: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"c2f852a9-e943-401f-9148-15e36a901ade","uuid":"13c99600-db21-4cf9-8857-8f5408d54603","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: ==========\\n2: Middleware\\n3: ==========\\n4: \\n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\\n6: It's a light, low-level \\\"plugin\\\" system for globally altering Django's input\\n7: or output.\\n8: \\n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\\n13: \\n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\\n18: \\n19: Writing your own middleware\\n20: ===========================\\n21: \\n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\\n25: \\n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\\n27: \\n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n30: \\n31:         def middleware(request):\\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n34: \\n35:             response = get_response(request)\\n36: \\n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n38:             # the view is called.\\n39: \\n40:             return response\\n41: \\n42:         return middleware\\n43: \\n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\\n45: \\n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\\n50: \\n51:         def __call__(self, request):\\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\\n54: \\n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\\n56: \\n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\\n58:             # the view is called.\\n59: \\n60:             return response\\n61: \\n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\\n66: \\n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\\n73: \\n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\\n77: \\n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\\n79: \\n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\\n81: --------------------------\\n82: \\n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\\n85: caveats:\\n86: \\n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\\n89: \\n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\\n92: \\n93: Marking middleware as unused\\n94: ----------------------------\\n95: \\n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\\n101: \\n102: Activating middleware\\n103: =====================\\n104: \\n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\\n106: your Django settings.\\n107: \\n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\\n112: \\n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\\n114:         \\\"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\\\",\\n115:         \\\"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\\\",\\n116:         \\\"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\\\",\\n117:         \\\"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\\\",\\n118:         \\\"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\\\",\\n119:         \\\"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\\\",\\n120:         \\\"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\\\",\\n121:     ]\\n122: \\n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\\n126: \\n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\\n128: other middleware. For instance,\\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\\n133: middleware classes.\\n134: \\n135: Middleware order and layering\\n136: =============================\\n137: \\n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\\n140: \\n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \\\"layer\\\" that\\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\\n146: out.\\n147: \\n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\\n152: \\n153: Other middleware hooks\\n154: ======================\\n155: \\n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\\n158: \\n159: .. _view-middleware:\\n160: \\n161: ``process_view()``\\n162: ------------------\\n163: \\n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\\n165: \\n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\\n172: (``request``).\\n173: \\n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\\n175: \\n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\\n182: \\n183: .. note::\\n184: \\n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\\n190: \\n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\\n196: \\n197: .. _exception-middleware:\\n198: \\n199: ``process_exception()``\\n200: -----------------------\\n201: \\n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\\n203: \\n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\\n206: \\n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\\n213: \\n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\\n218: \\n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\\n220: \\n221: ``process_template_response()``\\n222: -------------------------------\\n223: \\n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\\n225: \\n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\\n229: \\n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n233: \\n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\\n238: \\n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\\n241: called.\\n242: \\n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\\n245: \\n246: Dealing with streaming responses\\n247: ================================\\n248: \\n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\\n254: \\n255:     if response.streaming:\\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\\n257:     else:\\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\\n259: \\n260: .. note::\\n261: \\n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\\n265: \\n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\\n267:             for chunk in content:\\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\\n269: \\n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\\n274: support both types of iterator.\\n275: \\n276: Exception handling\\n277: ==================\\n278: \\n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\\n283: \\n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\\n294: \\n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\\n297: \\n298: .. _async-middleware:\\n299: \\n300: Asynchronous support\\n301: ====================\\n302: \\n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\\n306: \\n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\\n310: \\n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\\n313: \\n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\\n316: \\n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\\n322: \\n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\\n328: \\n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\\n332: \\n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\\n337: \\n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\\n339: \\n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\\n348: \\n349:             async def middleware(request):\\n350:                 # Do something here!\\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\\n352:                 return response\\n353: \\n354:         else:\\n355: \\n356:             def middleware(request):\\n357:                 # Do something here!\\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\\n359:                 return response\\n360: \\n361:         return middleware\\n362: \\n363: .. note::\\n364: \\n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\\n369: \\n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\\n372: \\n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\\n375: \\n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\\n377: \\n378: \\n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\\n380:         async_capable = True\\n381:         sync_capable = False\\n382: \\n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\\n387: \\n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\\n390:             # Some logic ...\\n391:             return response\\n392: \\n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\\n394: \\n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\\n396: ==========================================\\n397: \\n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\\n399:     :module:\\n400: \\n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\\n405: \\n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\\n408: \\n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\\n410: \\n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\\n413:    middleware and the view.\\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\\n415: #. Returns the response.\\n416: \\n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\\n419: directly.\\n420: \\n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\\n426: \\n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\\n429: \\n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\\n437:    response.\\n438: \\n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\\n445:    to the next middleware.\\n446: \\n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\\n455:    exception.\\n456: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: ==========\n2: Middleware\n3: ==========\n4: \n5: Middleware is a framework of hooks into Django's request/response processing.\n6: It's a light, low-level \"plugin\" system for globally altering Django's input\n7: or output.\n8: \n9: Each middleware component is responsible for doing some specific function. For\n10: example, Django includes a middleware component,\n11: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware`, that\n12: associates users with requests using sessions.\n13: \n14: This document explains how middleware works, how you activate middleware, and\n15: how to write your own middleware. Django ships with some built-in middleware\n16: you can use right out of the box. They're documented in the :doc:`built-in\n17: middleware reference </ref/middleware>`.\n18: \n19: Writing your own middleware\n20: ===========================\n21: \n22: A middleware factory is a callable that takes a ``get_response`` callable and\n23: returns a middleware. A middleware is a callable that takes a request and\n24: returns a response, just like a view.\n25: \n26: A middleware can be written as a function that looks like this::\n27: \n28:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\n29:         # One-time configuration and initialization.\n30: \n31:         def middleware(request):\n32:             # Code to be executed for each request before\n33:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\n34: \n35:             response = get_response(request)\n36: \n37:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\n38:             # the view is called.\n39: \n40:             return response\n41: \n42:         return middleware\n43: \n44: Or it can be written as a class whose instances are callable, like this::\n45: \n46:     class SimpleMiddleware:\n47:         def __init__(self, get_response):\n48:             self.get_response = get_response\n49:             # One-time configuration and initialization.\n50: \n51:         def __call__(self, request):\n52:             # Code to be executed for each request before\n53:             # the view (and later middleware) are called.\n54: \n55:             response = self.get_response(request)\n56: \n57:             # Code to be executed for each request/response after\n58:             # the view is called.\n59: \n60:             return response\n61: \n62: The ``get_response`` callable provided by Django might be the actual view (if\n63: this is the last listed middleware) or it might be the next middleware in the\n64: chain. The current middleware doesn't need to know or care what exactly it is,\n65: just that it represents whatever comes next.\n66: \n67: The above is a slight simplification -- the ``get_response`` callable for the\n68: last middleware in the chain won't be the actual view but rather a wrapper\n69: method from the handler which takes care of applying :ref:`view middleware\n70: <view-middleware>`, calling the view with appropriate URL arguments, and\n71: applying :ref:`template-response <template-response-middleware>` and\n72: :ref:`exception <exception-middleware>` middleware.\n73: \n74: Middleware can either support only synchronous Python (the default), only\n75: asynchronous Python, or both. See :ref:`async-middleware` for details of how to\n76: advertise what you support, and know what kind of request you are getting.\n77: \n78: Middleware can live anywhere on your Python path.\n79: \n80: ``__init__(get_response)``\n81: --------------------------\n82: \n83: Middleware factories must accept a ``get_response`` argument. You can also\n84: initialize some global state for the middleware. Keep in mind a couple of\n85: caveats:\n86: \n87: * Django initializes your middleware with only the ``get_response`` argument,\n88:   so you can't define ``__init__()`` as requiring any other arguments.\n89: \n90: * Unlike the ``__call__()`` method which is called once per request,\n91:   ``__init__()`` is called only *once*, when the web server starts.\n92: \n93: Marking middleware as unused\n94: ----------------------------\n95: \n96: It's sometimes useful to determine at startup time whether a piece of\n97: middleware should be used. In these cases, your middleware's ``__init__()``\n98: method may raise :exc:`~django.core.exceptions.MiddlewareNotUsed`. Django will\n99: then remove that middleware from the middleware process and log a debug message\n100: to the :ref:`django-request-logger` logger when :setting:`DEBUG` is ``True``.\n101: \n102: Activating middleware\n103: =====================\n104: \n105: To activate a middleware component, add it to the :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` list in\n106: your Django settings.\n107: \n108: In :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, each middleware component is represented by a string:\n109: the full Python path to the middleware factory's class or function name. For\n110: example, here's the default value created by :djadmin:`django-admin\n111: startproject <startproject>`::\n112: \n113:     MIDDLEWARE = [\n114:         \"django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware\",\n115:         \"django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware\",\n116:         \"django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware\",\n117:         \"django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware\",\n118:         \"django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware\",\n119:         \"django.contrib.messages.middleware.MessageMiddleware\",\n120:         \"django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware\",\n121:     ]\n122: \n123: A Django installation doesn't require any middleware — :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\n124: can be empty, if you'd like — but it's strongly suggested that you at least use\n125: :class:`~django.middleware.common.CommonMiddleware`.\n126: \n127: The order in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` matters because a middleware can depend on\n128: other middleware. For instance,\n129: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.middleware.AuthenticationMiddleware` stores the\n130: authenticated user in the session; therefore, it must run after\n131: :class:`~django.contrib.sessions.middleware.SessionMiddleware`. See\n132: :ref:`middleware-ordering` for some common hints about ordering of Django\n133: middleware classes.\n134: \n135: Middleware order and layering\n136: =============================\n137: \n138: During the request phase, before calling the view, Django applies middleware in\n139: the order it's defined in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, top-down.\n140: \n141: You can think of it like an onion: each middleware class is a \"layer\" that\n142: wraps the view, which is in the core of the onion. If the request passes\n143: through all the layers of the onion (each one calls ``get_response`` to pass\n144: the request in to the next layer), all the way to the view at the core, the\n145: response will then pass through every layer (in reverse order) on the way back\n146: out.\n147: \n148: If one of the layers decides to short-circuit and return a response without\n149: ever calling its ``get_response``, none of the layers of the onion inside that\n150: layer (including the view) will see the request or the response. The response\n151: will only return through the same layers that the request passed in through.\n152: \n153: Other middleware hooks\n154: ======================\n155: \n156: Besides the basic request/response middleware pattern described earlier, you\n157: can add three other special methods to class-based middleware:\n158: \n159: .. _view-middleware:\n160: \n161: ``process_view()``\n162: ------------------\n163: \n164: .. method:: process_view(request, view_func, view_args, view_kwargs)\n165: \n166: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``view_func`` is\n167: the Python function that Django is about to use. (It's the actual function\n168: object, not the name of the function as a string.) ``view_args`` is a list of\n169: positional arguments that will be passed to the view, and ``view_kwargs`` is a\n170: dictionary of keyword arguments that will be passed to the view. Neither\n171: ``view_args`` nor ``view_kwargs`` include the first view argument\n172: (``request``).\n173: \n174: ``process_view()`` is called just before Django calls the view.\n175: \n176: It should return either ``None`` or an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\n177: object. If it returns ``None``, Django will continue processing this request,\n178: executing any other ``process_view()`` middleware and, then, the appropriate\n179: view. If it returns an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, Django won't\n180: bother calling the appropriate view; it'll apply response middleware to that\n181: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` and return the result.\n182: \n183: .. note::\n184: \n185:     Accessing :attr:`request.POST <django.http.HttpRequest.POST>` inside\n186:     middleware before the view runs or in ``process_view()`` will prevent any\n187:     view running after the middleware from being able to :ref:`modify the\n188:     upload handlers for the request <modifying_upload_handlers_on_the_fly>`,\n189:     and should normally be avoided.\n190: \n191:     The :class:`~django.middleware.csrf.CsrfViewMiddleware` class can be\n192:     considered an exception, as it provides the\n193:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_exempt` and\n194:     :func:`~django.views.decorators.csrf.csrf_protect` decorators which allow\n195:     views to explicitly control at what point the CSRF validation should occur.\n196: \n197: .. _exception-middleware:\n198: \n199: ``process_exception()``\n200: -----------------------\n201: \n202: .. method:: process_exception(request, exception)\n203: \n204: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``exception`` is an\n205: ``Exception`` object raised by the view function.\n206: \n207: Django calls ``process_exception()`` when a view raises an exception.\n208: ``process_exception()`` should return either ``None`` or an\n209: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object. If it returns an\n210: :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse` object, the template response and response\n211: middleware will be applied and the resulting response returned to the\n212: browser. Otherwise, :ref:`default exception handling <error-views>` kicks in.\n213: \n214: Again, middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\n215: includes ``process_exception``. If an exception middleware returns a response,\n216: the ``process_exception`` methods of the middleware classes above that\n217: middleware won't be called at all.\n218: \n219: .. _template-response-middleware:\n220: \n221: ``process_template_response()``\n222: -------------------------------\n223: \n224: .. method:: process_template_response(request, response)\n225: \n226: ``request`` is an :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest` object. ``response`` is\n227: the :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` object (or equivalent)\n228: returned by a Django view or by a middleware.\n229: \n230: ``process_template_response()`` is called just after the view has finished\n231: executing, if the response instance has a ``render()`` method, indicating that\n232: it is a :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\n233: \n234: It must return a response object that implements a ``render`` method. It could\n235: alter the given ``response`` by changing ``response.template_name`` and\n236: ``response.context_data``, or it could create and return a brand-new\n237: :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse` or equivalent.\n238: \n239: You don't need to explicitly render responses -- responses will be\n240: automatically rendered once all template response middleware has been\n241: called.\n242: \n243: Middleware are run in reverse order during the response phase, which\n244: includes ``process_template_response()``.\n245: \n246: Dealing with streaming responses\n247: ================================\n248: \n249: Unlike :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`,\n250: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` does not have a ``content``\n251: attribute. As a result, middleware can no longer assume that all responses\n252: will have a ``content`` attribute. If they need access to the content, they\n253: must test for streaming responses and adjust their behavior accordingly::\n254: \n255:     if response.streaming:\n256:         response.streaming_content = wrap_streaming_content(response.streaming_content)\n257:     else:\n258:         response.content = alter_content(response.content)\n259: \n260: .. note::\n261: \n262:     ``streaming_content`` should be assumed to be too large to hold in memory.\n263:     Response middleware may wrap it in a new generator, but must not consume\n264:     it. Wrapping is typically implemented as follows::\n265: \n266:         def wrap_streaming_content(content):\n267:             for chunk in content:\n268:                 yield alter_content(chunk)\n269: \n270: :class:`~django.http.StreamingHttpResponse` allows both synchronous and\n271: asynchronous iterators. The wrapping function must match. Check\n272: :attr:`StreamingHttpResponse.is_async\n273: <django.http.StreamingHttpResponse.is_async>` if your middleware needs to\n274: support both types of iterator.\n275: \n276: Exception handling\n277: ==================\n278: \n279: Django automatically converts exceptions raised by the view or by middleware\n280: into an appropriate HTTP response with an error status code. :ref:`Certain\n281: exceptions <error-views>` are converted to 4xx status codes, while an unknown\n282: exception is converted to a 500 status code.\n283: \n284: This conversion takes place before and after each middleware (you can think of\n285: it as the thin film in between each layer of the onion), so that every\n286: middleware can always rely on getting some kind of HTTP response back from\n287: calling its ``get_response`` callable. Middleware don't need to worry about\n288: wrapping their call to ``get_response`` in a ``try/except`` and handling an\n289: exception that might have been raised by a later middleware or the view. Even\n290: if the very next middleware in the chain raises an\n291: :class:`~django.http.Http404` exception, for example, your middleware won't see\n292: that exception; instead it will get an :class:`~django.http.HttpResponse`\n293: object with a :attr:`~django.http.HttpResponse.status_code` of 404.\n294: \n295: You can set :setting:`DEBUG_PROPAGATE_EXCEPTIONS` to ``True`` to skip this\n296: conversion and propagate exceptions upward.\n297: \n298: .. _async-middleware:\n299: \n300: Asynchronous support\n301: ====================\n302: \n303: Middleware can support any combination of synchronous and asynchronous\n304: requests. Django will adapt requests to fit the middleware's requirements if it\n305: cannot support both, but at a performance penalty.\n306: \n307: By default, Django assumes that your middleware is capable of handling only\n308: synchronous requests. To change these assumptions, set the following attributes\n309: on your middleware factory function or class:\n310: \n311: * ``sync_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\n312:   synchronous requests. Defaults to ``True``.\n313: \n314: * ``async_capable`` is a boolean indicating if the middleware can handle\n315:   asynchronous requests. Defaults to ``False``.\n316: \n317: If your middleware has both ``sync_capable = True`` and\n318: ``async_capable = True``, then Django will pass it the request without\n319: converting it. In this case, you can work out if your middleware will receive\n320: async requests by checking if the ``get_response`` object you are passed is a\n321: coroutine function, using ``inspect.iscoroutinefunction``.\n322: \n323: The ``django.utils.decorators`` module contains\n324: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_only_middleware`,\n325: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.async_only_middleware`, and\n326: :func:`~django.utils.decorators.sync_and_async_middleware` decorators that\n327: allow you to apply these flags to middleware factory functions.\n328: \n329: The returned callable must match the sync or async nature of the\n330: ``get_response`` method. If you have an asynchronous ``get_response``, you must\n331: return a coroutine function (``async def``).\n332: \n333: ``process_view``, ``process_template_response`` and ``process_exception``\n334: methods, if they are provided, should also be adapted to match the sync/async\n335: mode. However, Django will individually adapt them as required if you do not,\n336: at an additional performance penalty.\n337: \n338: Here's an example of how to create a middleware function that supports both::\n339: \n340:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction\n341:     from django.utils.decorators import sync_and_async_middleware\n342: \n343: \n344:     @sync_and_async_middleware\n345:     def simple_middleware(get_response):\n346:         # One-time configuration and initialization goes here.\n347:         if iscoroutinefunction(get_response):\n348: \n349:             async def middleware(request):\n350:                 # Do something here!\n351:                 response = await get_response(request)\n352:                 return response\n353: \n354:         else:\n355: \n356:             def middleware(request):\n357:                 # Do something here!\n358:                 response = get_response(request)\n359:                 return response\n360: \n361:         return middleware\n362: \n363: .. note::\n364: \n365:     If you declare a hybrid middleware that supports both synchronous and\n366:     asynchronous calls, the kind of call you get may not match the underlying\n367:     view. Django will optimize the middleware call stack to have as few\n368:     sync/async transitions as possible.\n369: \n370:     Thus, even if you are wrapping an async view, you may be called in sync\n371:     mode if there is other, synchronous middleware between you and the view.\n372: \n373: When using an asynchronous class-based middleware, you must ensure that\n374: instances are correctly marked as coroutine functions::\n375: \n376:     from inspect import iscoroutinefunction, markcoroutinefunction\n377: \n378: \n379:     class AsyncMiddleware:\n380:         async_capable = True\n381:         sync_capable = False\n382: \n383:         def __init__(self, get_response):\n384:             self.get_response = get_response\n385:             if iscoroutinefunction(self.get_response):\n386:                 markcoroutinefunction(self)\n387: \n388:         async def __call__(self, request):\n389:             response = await self.get_response(request)\n390:             # Some logic ...\n391:             return response\n392: \n393: .. _upgrading-middleware:\n394: \n395: Upgrading pre-Django 1.10-style middleware\n396: ==========================================\n397: \n398: .. class:: django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin\n399:     :module:\n400: \n401: Django provides ``django.utils.deprecation.MiddlewareMixin`` to ease creating\n402: middleware classes that are compatible with both :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and the\n403: old ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, and support synchronous and asynchronous requests.\n404: All middleware classes included with Django are compatible with both settings.\n405: \n406: The mixin provides an ``__init__()`` method that requires a ``get_response``\n407: argument and stores it in ``self.get_response``.\n408: \n409: The ``__call__()`` method:\n410: \n411: #. Calls ``self.process_request(request)`` (if defined).\n412: #. Calls ``self.get_response(request)`` to get the response from later\n413:    middleware and the view.\n414: #. Calls ``self.process_response(request, response)`` (if defined).\n415: #. Returns the response.\n416: \n417: If used with ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, the ``__call__()`` method will\n418: never be used; Django calls ``process_request()`` and ``process_response()``\n419: directly.\n420: \n421: In most cases, inheriting from this mixin will be sufficient to make an\n422: old-style middleware compatible with the new system with sufficient\n423: backwards-compatibility. The new short-circuiting semantics will be harmless or\n424: even beneficial to the existing middleware. In a few cases, a middleware class\n425: may need some changes to adjust to the new semantics.\n426: \n427: These are the behavioral differences between using :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` and\n428: ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``:\n429: \n430: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, every middleware will always have its\n431:    ``process_response`` method called, even if an earlier middleware\n432:    short-circuited by returning a response from its ``process_request``\n433:    method. Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, middleware behaves more like an onion:\n434:    the layers that a response goes through on the way out are the same layers\n435:    that saw the request on the way in. If a middleware short-circuits, only\n436:    that middleware and the ones before it in :setting:`MIDDLEWARE` will see the\n437:    response.\n438: \n439: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, ``process_exception`` is applied to\n440:    exceptions raised from a middleware ``process_request`` method. Under\n441:    :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, ``process_exception`` applies only to exceptions\n442:    raised from the view (or from the ``render`` method of a\n443:    :class:`~django.template.response.TemplateResponse`). Exceptions raised from\n444:    a middleware are converted to the appropriate HTTP response and then passed\n445:    to the next middleware.\n446: \n447: #. Under ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES``, if a ``process_response`` method raises\n448:    an exception, the ``process_response`` methods of all earlier middleware are\n449:    skipped and a ``500 Internal Server Error`` HTTP response is always\n450:    returned (even if the exception raised was e.g. an\n451:    :class:`~django.http.Http404`). Under :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`, an exception\n452:    raised from a middleware will immediately be converted to the appropriate\n453:    HTTP response, and then the next middleware in line will see that\n454:    response. Middleware are never skipped due to a middleware raising an\n455:    exception.\n456: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/http/middleware.txt"}}}

Loads and constructs the middleware chain\n- `BaseHandler.adapt_method_mode()` - Adapts middleware methods between sync/async\n- `BaseHandler.get_response()` - Synchronous request handler\n\n\n- `BaseHandler.get_response_async()` - New-style middleware base class\n- `MiddlewareMixin.__call__()` - Invokes middleware in sync context\n- `MiddlewareMixin.__acall__()` - Invokes middleware in async context\n\n## Chain of Execution:\n\nMiddleware gets assembled in `BaseHandler.load_middleware()` by iterating through `settings.MIDDLEWARE` backwards, instantiating each with a `get_response` callable that wraps the previous layer. This creates a nested structure where the outermost middleware wraps all others, forming a chain stored in `self._middleware_chain`.\n\nWhen a request arrives, `BaseHandler.get_response()` invokes `self._middleware_chain(request)`, which passes the request through each middleware layer sequentially.\n\nEach middleware can intercept the request before calling `get_response()` to advance to the next layer, then intercept the response on the way back. The innermost handler processes view middleware, executes the view, handles template response middleware, and manages exceptions. Responses bubble back through the middleware stack in reverse order.\n\nCompared to WSGI middleware, Django's approach creates an onion-like structure where middleware wraps each other. A key distinction is that when middleware short-circuits by returning early, only that middleware and preceding ones process the response—subsequent layers never see through a sophisticated stacking mechanism that creates a callable chain resembling an onion. The core implementation is located in django/core/handlers/base.py, specifically in the BaseHandler class which is inherited by both WSGIHandler (django/core/handlers/wsgi.py) and ASGIHandler (django/core/handlers/asgi.py).\\n\\nMIDDLEWARE STACK CONSTRUCTION:\\nThe BaseHandler.load_middleware() method (base.py:27-104) processes the MIDDLEWARE setting by iterating through it in REVERSE order. This reverse iteration is crucial: each middleware is instantiated with the previous handler as its get_response callable, creating a nested chain where the first middleware in settings.MIDDLEWARE ends up as the outermost layer, wrapping all others. The method instantiates middleware classes by calling middleware_class(adapted_handler), where adapted_handler is the previously constructed handler. Each middleware instance is wrapped with convert_exception_to_response() (exception.py:25-61) to ensure exceptions are immediately converted to HTTP responses rather than propagating. The final chain is stored in self._middleware_chain.\\n\\nREQUEST/RESPONSE FLOW:\\nWhen a request arrives, get_response() (base.py:138-152) is called, which delegates to self._middleware_chain(request). This chain execution passes through all middleware in order (outermost first), allowing each to execute process_request logic before calling get_response() to pass control inward. The core view handler, _get_response() (base.py:176-228), handles view middleware, the actual view execution, template response middleware, and exception middleware. As the response travels back outward through the middleware chain, each middleware can execute process_response logic.\\n\\nKEY DIFFERENCES FROM WSGI MIDDLEWARE STACKING:\\nDjango's new MIDDLEWARE behaves like an onion where middleware layers wrap each other through callable nesting, similar to WSGI applications. However, there are critical differences: (1) If a middleware short-circuits by returning a response without calling get_response(), only that middleware and those outside it in the chain see the response—inner middleware and the view are never called. (2) Exception handling is integrated into each layer via convert_exception_to_response() wrappers, so exceptions are immediately converted to responses and passed to the next middleware, rather than potentially skipping middleware. (3) The process_request/process_response execution order follows the onion pattern: request phase goes inward (top to bottom of MIDDLEWARE list), response phase goes outward (bottom to top), with short-circuiting naturally interrupting the onion's interior layers.\\n\\nASYNC/SYNC ADAPTATION:\\nDjango's handlers support both synchronous and asynchronous middleware through the adapt_method_mode() method (base.py:106-136), which wraps sync methods with sync_to_async or async methods with async_to_sync as needed. The MiddlewareMixin class (deprecation.py:276-332) provides a base for middleware supporting both sync and async via __call__ and __acall__ methods.\\n\\nEXCEPTION HANDLING:\\nThe convert_exception_to_response() wrapper in exception.py ensures that no middleware or handler ever leaks an exception—all exceptions are caught and converted to appropriate HTTP responses (4xx for known exceptions, 500 for others) by response_for_exception(). This is wrapped around each middleware and the core handler, creating an exception-safety guarantee.\\n\\nThe WSGI and ASGI handlers (wsgi.py and asgi.py) both call load_middleware() in their constructors to build this chain at startup, allowing Django to efficiently serve requests by executing a prebuilt middleware chain.\"\n}\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":9,"cache_creation_input_tokens":6205,"cache_read_input_tokens":60654,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":6205},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"c2f852a9-e943-401f-9148-15e36a901ade","uuid":"17426f59-d590-4743-b1d4-c7b15149520c"}

through a sophisticated stacking mechanism that creates a callable chain resembling an onion. The core implementation is located in django/core/handlers/base.py, specifically in the BaseHandler class which is inherited by both WSGIHandler (django/core/handlers/wsgi.py) and ASGIHandler (django/core/handlers/asgi.py).\\n\\nMIDDLEWARE STACK CONSTRUCTION:\\nThe BaseHandler.load_middleware() method (base.py:27-104) processes the MIDDLEWARE setting by iterating through it in REVERSE order. This reverse iteration is crucial: each middleware is instantiated with the previous handler as its get_response callable, creating a nested chain where the first middleware in settings.MIDDLEWARE ends up as the outermost layer, wrapping all others. The method instantiates middleware classes by calling middleware_class(adapted_handler), where adapted_handler is the previously constructed handler. Each middleware instance is wrapped with convert_exception_to_response() (exception.py:25-61) to ensure exceptions are immediately converted to HTTP responses rather than propagating. As the response travels back outward through the middleware chain, each middleware can execute process_response logic.\\n\\nKEY DIFFERENCES FROM WSGI MIDDLEWARE STACKING:\\nDjango's new MIDDLEWARE behaves like an onion where middleware layers wrap each other through callable nesting, similar to WSGI applications. However, there are critical differences: (1) If a middleware short-circuits by returning a response without calling get_response(), only that middleware and those outside it in the chain see the response—inner middleware and the view are never called. (2) Exception handling is integrated into each layer via convert_exception_to_response() wrappers, so exceptions are immediately converted to responses and passed to the next middleware, rather than potentially skipping middleware. (3) The process_request/process_response execution order follows the onion pattern: request phase goes inward (top to bottom of MIDDLEWARE list), response phase goes outward (bottom to top), with short-circuiting naturally interrupting the onion's interior layers.\\n\\nASYNC/SYNC ADAPTATION:\\nDjango's handlers support both synchronous and asynchronous middleware through the adapt_method_mode() method (base.py:106-136), which wraps sync methods with sync_to_async or async methods with async_to_sync as needed. This is wrapped around each middleware and the core handler, creating an exception-safety guarantee.\\n\\nThe WSGI and ASGI handlers (wsgi.py and asgi.py) both call load_middleware() in their constructors to build this chain at startup, allowing Django to efficiently serve requests by executing a prebuilt middleware chain.\"\n}\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}

The core implementation is located in django/core/handlers/base.py, specifically in the BaseHandler class which is inherited by both WSGIHandler (django/core/handlers/wsgi.py) and ASGIHandler (django/core/handlers/asgi.py).\\n\\nMIDDLEWARE STACK CONSTRUCTION:\\nThe BaseHandler.load_middleware() method (base.py:27-104) processes the MIDDLEWARE setting by iterating through it in REVERSE order. This reverse iteration is crucial: each middleware is instantiated with the previous handler as its get_response callable, creating a nested chain where the first middleware in settings.MIDDLEWARE ends up as the outermost layer, wrapping all others. (3) The process_request/process_response execution order follows the onion pattern: request phase goes inward (top to bottom of MIDDLEWARE list), response phase goes outward (bottom to top), with short-circuiting naturally interrupting the onion's interior layers.\\n\\nASYNC/SYNC ADAPTATION:\\nDjango's handlers support both synchronous and asynchronous middleware through the adapt_method_mode() method (base.py:106-136), which wraps sync methods with sync_to_async or async methods with async_to_sync as needed. This is wrapped around each middleware and the core handler, creating an exception-safety guarantee.\\n\\nThe WSGI and ASGI handlers (wsgi.py and asgi.py) both call load_middleware() in their constructors to build this chain at startup, allowing Django to efficiently serve requests by executing a prebuilt middleware chain.\"\n}\n","numLines":143,"startLine":1,"totalLines":143}}}
